In the immediate aftermath, [[Leader of the Opposition (UK)|Leader of the Opposition]] [[Jeremy Corbyn]] called for a [[2019 motion of no confidence in the May ministry|vote of no confidence in the government]], which was held on 16 January 2019. The government won the vote by 325 to 306, a majority of 19.<ref>{{Cite news | last = Staff writer |title=May's government survives no-confidence vote |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-46899466|work=BBC News|date=16 January 2019|access-date=23 January 2019|language=en-GB}}</ref>

===Vote on "motion in neutral terms" (29 January 2019)===
Section 13 of the 2018 Act required that the government put down a motion, in neutral terms, in response to the written statements made by the prime minister on 21 and 24 January, in which she set out her "Plan B". In accordance with Dominic Grieve's "three day amendment" to the parliamentary timetable, this motion was tabled on 21 January (three sitting days after the draft Withdrawal Agreement was rejected by MPs), and it was put to a vote on 29 January 2019. It took the form of a resolution to the effect that the House of Commons had "considered" the Prime Minister's statements. This neutral motion was subject to amendment, and prior to voting on the main motion, the Commons voted on seven amendments, proposed by MPs and selected by the Speaker.<ref>[https://www.standard.co.uk/news/politics/brexit-news-latest-commons-showdown-looms-for-theresa-may-as-mps-bid-to-change-course-of-eu-exit-a4051271.html "Brexit amendments: The key amendments to Theresa May's deal that MPs voted on tonight"], ''Evening Standard'', 29 January 2019</ref>

Two amendments were passed. The Brady amendment called on the Government to re-negotiate over the [[Northern Ireland backstop]]. It passed by 16 votes, supported by the Conservatives and DUP over other parties in the Commons, but with 7 Labour MPs supporting it and 8 Conservative MPs voting against it. The Spelman-Dromey amendment declared the desire of the Commons to avoid a no-deal Brexit. It passed by 8 votes, supported by all the parties other than the Conservatives and DUP, but with the support of 17 Conservative MPs. An amendment seeking to pave the way for a binding legislation that would prevent no deal, the Cooper-Boles amendment, failed by 23 votes. Three other amendments also failed.<ref>{{cite news | last = Staff writer |title= Brexit amendments: What did MPs vote on and what were the results? |url= https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-46959545| work = [[BBC News]] |date=29 January 2019 |access-date=29 January 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite web | last = Lovegrove | first = Simon |title= United Kingdom (and EU regulation): MPs reject all but two amendments to Theresa May's Brexit plan | website = regulationtomorrow.com/eu | publisher = Financial services: Regulation tomorrow |url= https://www.regulationtomorrow.com/eu/mps-reject-all-but-two-amendments-to-theresa-mays-brexit-plan/ |date=29 January 2019 |access-date=29 January 2019}}</ref> The main motion (as amended) was then passed without a division.

{{collapse top|title=Final full text of resolution of the House of Commons on 29 January 2019}}
:Resolved, '''That this House''', in accordance with the provisions of section 13(6)(a) and 13(11)(b)(i) and 13(13)(b) of the European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018, '''has considered the Written Statement''' titled "Statement under Section 13(4) of the European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018" and '''made on 21 January 2019''', '''and the Written Statement''' titled "Statement under Section 13(11)(a) of the European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018" and '''made on 24 January 2019, and rejects the United Kingdom leaving the European Union without a Withdrawal Agreement and a Framework for the Future Relationship, and requires the Northern Ireland backstop to be replaced with alternative arrangements to avoid a hard border; supports leaving the European Union with a deal and would therefore support the Withdrawal Agreement subject to this change'''.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://hansard.parliament.uk/commons/2019-01-29/debates/BB8A5769-12B4-4D0E-9B4E-158F89F9FCDE/EuropeanUnion(Withdrawal)Act2018%7C|title=European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018 - Hansard|website=hansard.parliament.uk}}</ref>
{{collapse bottom}}

May stated that a further "meaningful vote" would be held "as soon as we possibly can", but that if it did not take place by 13 February then she would present a statement, to be followed by a debate on an amendable motion on 14 February.<ref>{{cite hansard|house=[[House of Commons of the United Kingdom|House of Commons]]|url=https://hansard.parliament.uk/Commons/2019-01-29/debates/BB8A5769-12B4-4D0E-9B4E-158F89F9FCDE/EuropeanUnion(Withdrawal)Act2018#contribution-297C69CC-2919-4BE6-88E4-A2AA3721682D |date=29 January 2019 |title= European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2018 |column= 671 |speaker=[[Theresa May]] |position=[[Prime Minister of the United Kingdom|Prime Minister]]|quote=First of all, as I have said, we will bring a revised deal back to this House for a second meaningful vote as soon as we possibly can. While we will want the House to support that deal, if it did not, we would—just as before—table an amendable motion for debate the next day. Furthermore, if we have not brought a revised deal back to this House by Wednesday 13 February, we will make a statement and, again, table an amendable motion for debate the next day.}}</ref>

===Vote on amendable motion (14 February 2019)===
On 12 February 2019, Theresa May made a statement to the House of Commons on the government's progress in securing a withdrawal agreement.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.gov.uk/government/speeches/pms-statement-to-the-house-of-commons-on-brexit-12-february-2019|title=PM's statement to the House of Commons on Brexit: 12 February 2019|website=GOV.UK}}</ref> This was followed, on 14 February, by a vote on an amendable motion in the following terms: "That this House welcomes the Prime Minister’s statement of 12 February 2019; reiterates its support for the approach to leaving the EU expressed by this House on 29 January 2019 and notes that discussions between the UK and the EU on the Northern Ireland backstop are ongoing."'

All proposed amendments to the motion were defeated. The motion itself was then defeated, by a margin of 303-259, due in part to abstentions by the European Research Group faction of Conservative MPs, who objected that the motion appeared to rule out leaving the EU without a withdrawal agreement.<ref>{{cite news | last = Staff writer |title= Brexit amendments: What did MPs vote on and what were the results? |url= https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-47243407| work = [[BBC News]] |date=14 February 2019 |access-date=15 February 2019}}</ref>

In her statement on 12 February, the prime minister reiterated her goal of having a second "meaningful vote" on a withdrawal agreement. She indicated that if this was not achieved by 26 February the government would make another statement to the House on the government's progress, and table an amendable motion relating to that statement, which would be put to a vote on 27 February.

===Vote on amendable motion (27 February 2019)===
On 27 February 2019, the House of Commons voted on an amendable motion in the following terms: "That this House notes the Prime Minister’s statement on Leaving the European Union of 26 February 2019; and further notes that discussions between the UK and the EU are ongoing."

Two amendments were passed.  An amendment to seek a joint UK-EU commitment to citizens' rights was passed without a division.  An amendment to specify the timeframe for further meaningful votes in March 2019 was passed by a margin of 502–20.  The main motion (as amended) was then passed without a division.<ref name="Hansard_27Feb2019">{{cite hansard|house=[[House of Commons of the United Kingdom|House of Commons]]|url=https://hansard.parliament.uk/commons/2019-02-27/debates/78B98FAF-F95B-42F6-848F-5047AEAC46A8/UK%E2%80%99SWithdrawalFromTheEU |date=27 February 2019 |title= UK's Withdrawal from the EU }}</ref>

{{collapse top|title=Final full text of resolution of the House of Commons on 27 February 2019}}
:Resolved, That this House notes the Prime Minister’s statement on Leaving the European Union of 26 February 2019; and further notes that discussions between the UK and the EU are ongoing; and requires the Prime Minister to seek at the earliest opportunity a joint UK-EU commitment to adopt part two of the Withdrawal Agreement on Citizens’ Rights and ensure its implementation prior to the UK’s exiting the European Union, whatever the outcome of negotiations on other aspects of the Withdrawal Agreement; and further notes in particular the commitment of the Prime Minister made in this House to hold a second meaningful vote by 12 March and if the House, having rejected leaving with the deal negotiated with the EU, then rejects leaving on 29 March without a withdrawal agreement and future framework, the Government will, on 14 March, bring forward a motion on whether Parliament wants to seek a short limited extension to Article 50, and if the House votes for an extension, seek to agree that extension approved by the House with the EU, and bring forward the necessary legislation to change the exit date commensurate with that extension.<ref name="Hansard_27Feb2019" />
{{collapse bottom}}

=== Second "meaningful vote" (12 March 2019) ===
The second meaningful vote took place on 12 March 2019.<ref>{{cite web |title= Parliamentary business for Tuesday 12 March 2019 |url= http://services.parliament.uk/Calendar/Commons/MainChamber/2019/03/12/events.html | website = services.parliament.uk | publisher = [[UK Parliament]] |date=7 March 2019 |access-date=8 March 2019}}</ref> The deal was supported by 235 Conservative MPs, four independent MPs, and Labour MPs [[Kevin Barron]], [[Caroline Flint]] and [[John Mann (British politician)|John Mann]], and was opposed by the remaining MPs, including all 10 DUP MPs and 75 Conservative MPs.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-47547887|title=MPs reject May's EU withdrawal deal again|date=13 March 2019|access-date=13 March 2019|language=en-GB}}</ref> One Conservative MP, [[Douglas Ross (Scottish politician)|Douglas Ross]], was unable to vote or exercise his right to a proxy vote due to the birth of his child on the day of the vote.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.pressandjournal.co.uk/fp/news/moray/1697987/moray-mp-to-miss-brexit-vote-to-be-by-wifes-side-as-she-goes-into-labour/|title=Moray MP to miss Brexit vote to be by wife's side as she goes into labour|last=Rougvie|first=Frances|website=Press and Journal|language=en-US|access-date=13 March 2019}}</ref><ref name="Hansard_354">{{cite hansard|house=[[House of Commons of the United Kingdom|House of Commons]]|url= https://hansard.parliament.uk/Commons/2019-03-12/division/4F5EA64F-7278-4AA4-BB58-E7FE3B3EF1A3/EuropeanUnion(Withdrawal)Act?outputType=Party |date=12 March 2019 |title= European Union (Withdrawal) Bill: Division 354}}</ref>

It was also noted that ''[[Holby City]]'' episode ''[[A Simple Lie]] Part 1'' was delayed on BBC One for a week for coverage of the vote.

=== Vote on "no deal" (13 March 2019) ===
[[File:Westminster & Brexit (33495857668).jpg|thumb|upright|Protesters outside the [[Palace of Westminster]], shortly before the vote on "no deal" on 13 March 2019]]
Under the terms of the 27 February motion, the defeat of the second meaningful vote means that the Government must promptly bring forward a motion regarding leaving the European Union without a withdrawal agreement. The motion, which blocked a no-deal Brexit, was presented on 13 March.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.ft.com/content/2f6ef290-458b-11e9-a965-23d669740bfb|title=Theresa May plans to vote to block no-deal Brexit|author=Laura Hughes|work=Financial Times|date=13 March 2019|access-date=13 March 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://services.parliament.uk/Calendar/Commons/MainChamber/2019/03/13/events.html|title=Parliamentary business for 13 March|website=services.parliament.uk|language=en|access-date=13 March 2019}}</ref> Two amendments to the motion were voted upon: the first, tabled by [[Caroline Spelman]] and categorically rejecting no-deal in any circumstances, passed 312–308; the second, the "[[Kit Malthouse|Malthouse]] compromise" supporting a so-called "managed no-deal Brexit", failed 164–374.

=== Vote on extending Article 50 (14 March 2019) ===
As leaving the European Union without a deal was rejected, then the Government was required to bring to 14 March sitting a motion regarding extending Article 50 past 29 March.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.parliament.uk/business/news/2019/march/key-brexit-vote-as-meaningful-vote-returns-to-the-commons/|title=Key Brexit vote as 'meaningful vote' returns to the Commons - News from Parliament|website=UK Parliament|language=en|access-date=13 March 2019}}</ref>

The motion as tabled by the Government states that if the Withdrawal Agreement had not been ratified by 20 March, then the Government would seek an extension of Article 50 to 30 June, the last possible day that Brexit could take place without requiring British participation in [[2019 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom|May's European elections]].<ref name="BBC_47564793">{{Cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-47564793|title=PM to bring third Brexit deal vote to MPs|date=14 March 2019|access-date=14 March 2019|language=en-GB}}</ref>

Four amendments were selected for debate alongside the motion:<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.instituteforgovernment.org.uk/explainers/parliament-next-steps-brexit|title=Parliament's next steps on Brexit|date=23 January 2019|website=The Institute for Government|language=en|access-date=14 March 2019}}</ref>
* Amendment (h) tabled by Sarah Wollaston that removes the 30 June deadline and instructs the government to seek an extension to enable a [[Referendum on the Brexit withdrawal agreement|second referendum]] was rejected by 85–334.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/av/uk-politics-47575445/mps-reject-a-second-brexit-referendum-by-334-votes-to-85|title=MPs reject a second Brexit referendum by 334 votes to 85|publisher=BBC|date=14 March 2019|access-date=15 March 2019}}</ref> 
* Amendment (i) tabled by Hilary Benn that calls for an Article 50 extension and allows backbenchers to set the business of the House on 20 March 2019 was rejected by 312-314.
** An amendment to the amendment tabled by Lucy Powell, which set the Article 50 extension to 30 June, was rejected by 311–314.
* Amendment (e) tabled by the Labour leadership that removes the 30 June deadline and instructs the government to enable a different approach to negotiations was rejected by 302–318.
* Amendment (j) tabled by Chris Bryant that prevents a third meaningful vote was not moved to a vote.

The main motion was approved by 412–202.

=== Vote on first Letwin amendment and Beckett amendment (25 March 2019) ===
Tabled by [[Oliver Letwin]], the amendment passed 329–302 and required the Commons to hold a series of indicative votes on 27 March.  Three ministers resigned from the government to support the amendment: [[Richard Harrington (politician)|Richard Harrington]] (business minister), [[Alistair Burt]] (foreign office), and [[Steve Brine]] (health). The Beckett amendment, tabled by [[Margaret Beckett]], was defeated 314–311. It would have required Parliament to vote favourably for a "no deal" Brexit or request an extension to Article 50 if the government was within seven days of leaving the European Union without a deal.  The amended main motion (Letwin but not Beckett) passed 327–300.

=== First Round of Indicative Votes (27 March 2019) ===
As a result of the first Letwin amendment's success, indicative votes on Parliament's preferred Brexit options were held on 27 March.  Eight propositions were voted upon, of which all eight failed.

In a departure from Westminster parliamentary convention, all eight votes took place simultaneously, using ballot papers, rather than having MPs walk through lobbies to signify their vote.

=== Third "meaningful vote" (29 March 2019) ===
As parliament had agreed to an extension of Article 50 to 30 June the possibility of a third meaningful vote was raised<ref name="BBC_47564793" /> and the Speaker ruled that "the same proposition or substantially the same proposition" could not be brought back in the same parliamentary session.<ref>{{cite hansard|house=[[House of Commons of the United Kingdom|House of Commons]]|url=https://hansard.parliament.uk/Commons/2019-03-18/debates/AB031E78-C906-4833-9ACF-291998FAC0E1/Speaker%E2%80%99SStatement#debate-3728111 |date=18 March 2019 |title= Speaker’s Statement |volume= 656 |speaker=[[John Bercow]] |position=[[Speaker of the House of Commons (United Kingdom)|Speaker of the House of Commons]]}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|last=Elgot|first=Jessica |title=Brexit: John Bercow rules out third meaningful vote on same deal|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/mar/18/brexit-john-bercow-rules-out-third-meaningful-vote-on-same-deal | work=The Guardian|date=18 March 2019 |access-date=12 April 2019}}</ref> The Political Declaration was then removed from the ballot in order to make it a different proposition.<ref>{{cite web|last=Elliott|first=Mark |title=Meaningful Vote 3: The legal implications of separating the Withdrawal Agreement and the Political Declaration|url=https://publiclawforeveryone.com/2019/03/28/meaningful-vote-3-the-legal-implications-of-separating-the-withdrawal-agreement-and-the-political-declaration/ |website=publiclawforeveryone.com|publisher=Public Law for Everyone |date=28 March 2019 |access-date=12 April 2019}}</ref>

The third vote meaningful vote on the Withdrawal Agreement was then able to take place on 29 March 2019.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://services.parliament.uk/Calendar/Commons/MainChamber/2019/03/29/events.html|title=Parliament UK: Parliamentary Calendar|website=services.parliament.uk|language=en|access-date=29 March 2019}}</ref> May promised to resign as Prime Minister if the Withdrawal Agreement was passed.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-47742395|title=MPs voting on Brexit delay plan|work=BBC News|date=29 March 2019|access-date=29 March 2019|language=en-GB}}</ref> In the end, May's deal was voted down again, albeit by a smaller margin than in the previous two votes.<ref name="Hansard_395">{{cite hansard|house=[[House of Commons of the United Kingdom|House of Commons]]|url= https://hansard.parliament.uk/Commons/2019-03-29/division/B6052BBD-43BE-4A30-8365-E3A8B108009E/UnitedKingdom%E2%80%99SWithdrawalFromTheEuropeanUnion?outputType=Party |date=29 March 2019 |title= European Union (Withdrawal) Bill: Division 354}}</ref>

===Second Round of Indicative Votes (1 April 2019)===
Further indicative votes were held on 1 April on propositions chosen by the [[Speaker of the House of Commons (United Kingdom)|Speaker]].<ref name="1April8Options">{{cite web|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-47767627|title=Brexit: What are MPs voting on?|website=[[BBC News Online|BBC.co.uk/News]]|date=1 April 2019|access-date=1 April 2019|df=dmy-all}}</ref><ref name="1April4Options">{{cite web|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-47776505|title=Brexit: Labour to support Norway-style EU proposal|website=[[BBC News Online|BBC.co.uk/News]]|date=1 April 2019|access-date=1 April 2019|df=dmy-all}}</ref>

Once again, in a departure from parliamentary convention, rather than having MPs walk through lobbies to indicate their vote choice, all four votes took place simultaneously using ballot papers.<ref>{{cite web |last1=Odell |first1=Mark |title=Speaker calls on MPs to vote |url=https://www.ft.com/content/3e128714-2db8-3286-8b30-f0ffa6661e26#post-24335 |website=Financial Times |access-date=2 April 2019 |date=1 April 2019}}</ref>

=== Cooper–Letwin Bill (3 April 2019) ===
{{main|European Union (Withdrawal) Act 2019}}
Since none of the tabled propositions in the second round of indicative votes could command a majority in the House of Commons, a third round of indicative votes was planned to be held on 3 April.<ref>{{cite news |url = https://www.politico.eu/article/house-of-commons-rejects-all-brexit-options-again/ |title = House of Commons Rejects All Brexit Options - Again |date = 1 April 2019 |publisher = Politico Europe |access-date = 2 April 2019}}</ref> On the day of 3 April 2019, the House of Commons focused instead on debating the "European Union (Withdrawal) (No. 5) Bill". This bill was otherwise known as the Cooper–Letwin Bill, after its chief sponsors [[Yvette Cooper]] (Labour) and [[Oliver Letwin]] (Conservative). The bill places an obligation on the Government to seek consent for any or no extensions to the date of withdrawal from the EU. To do so, the House of Commons first debated a business of the House motion to allow the Bill to be brought in for debate on that day. There was one proposed amendment to the Business of the House motion, which would have sought to schedule more indicative votes for 8 April 2019; this failed in the first tied vote since [[Maastricht Rebels|1993]].<ref name="APAvert">{{cite web|url=https://www.apnews.com/e6521ffd590e4b69bf023bbe3025e2a5|title=The Latest: UK lawmakers vote to avert 'no-deal' Brexit|website=APNews.com|publisher=Associated Press|date=3 April 2019|access-date=11 April 2019|df=dmy-all}}</ref>

{| class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; font-size:98%;"

| colspan="3"  style="text-align:center; background:silver;"|'''Further indicative votes'''

| colspan="3" style="text-align:left;" | Sources: [[Reuters]],<ref name="03-04ExtendIndicative"/> [[House of Commons of the United Kingdom|CommonsVotes]]<ref name="ComVotes1">{{cite web|url=https://commonsvotes.digiminster.com/Divisions/Details/671|title=Amendment (a) to the Business of the House motion|website=CommonsVotes.DigiMinister.com|date=3 April 2019|access-date=3 April 2019|df=dmy-all}}</ref>
|}

The tie was broken by the Speaker in favour of "No" (in accordance with [[Speaker Denison's rule]]), meaning the motion was rejected. The lack of passage of this motion meant that no further indicative votes would be scheduled by the House of Commons to be held on 8 April 2019.<ref name="03-04ExtendIndicative">{{cite web|url=https://uk.reuters.com/article/uk-britain-eu-votes/british-parliament-rejects-plan-to-hold-more-brexit-indicative-votes-on-monday-idUKKCN1RF26X|title=British parliament rejects plan to hold more Brexit indicative votes on Monday|last1=James|first1=William|last2=MacLellan|first2=Kylie|last3=MacAskill|first3=Andrew|website=[[Reuters.com]]|date=3 April 2019|access-date=3 April 2019|df=dmy-all}}</ref>

After losing the third vote and the approval of the Cooper–Letwin Bill at the third reading by 313-312, May and her cabinet considered the possibility of bringing the withdrawal agreement back to parliament for a fourth vote.<ref>{{cite news|last1=Stewart|first1=Heather|last2=Elgot|first2=Jessica|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/mar/29/mps-reject-theresa-mays-brexit-deal-third-time|title=May hopes to hold fourth vote on Brexit deal|date=29 March 2019|newspaper=The Guardian|access-date=29 March 2019}}</ref> In mid-May, May said that she would bring the withdrawal agreement to the parliament in the first week on June.<ref>{{cite news|last=Elwazer|first=Schams|url=https://edition.cnn.com/2019/05/14/europe/brexit-june-3-theresa-may-intl/index.html|title=Theresa May will put her Brexit deal to a vote—for the fourth time|date=14 May 2019|publisher=CNN|access-date=15 May 2019}}</ref> Due to huge opposition to the new agreement, May postponed the publication from 24 May to 4 June, and subsequently [[Theresa May#Resignation|resigned as prime minister]].<ref>{{cite news|url=http://en.rfi.fr/europe/20190523-brexit-vote-uk-parliament-delayed-until-4-june-government|title=Brexit vote in UK parliament delayed until 4 June|date=23 May 2019|publisher=Radio France Internationale|access-date=23 May 2019}}</ref>

===Prorogation of parliament===
{{main|2019 British prorogation controversy}}
[[Boris Johnson]], [[2019 Conservative Party leadership election|elected Conservative Party leader]] in July, and invited by the Queen to become Prime Minister, announced on 28 August that he had requested the Queen to [[Prorogation in the United Kingdom|prorogue]] Parliament from between 9 September and 12 September until the beginning of a new session on 14 October. On the advice of the (minimally [[quorum|quorate]]) [[Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council|Privy Council]] and in accordance with constitutional convention, the Queen granted this request the same day.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/live/2019/aug/28/spending-review-set-for-next-week-fuels-election-speculation-live?page=with%3Ablock-5d668a668f08ea59f447a9f1|title=Suspension of parliament: MPs react with fury and Davidson set to quit after Johnson move – as it happened|last1=Perraudin|first1=Frances|date=2019-08-28|work=The Guardian|access-date=2019-09-03|last2=Otte (later)|first2=Ben Quinn (earlier); Jedidajah|language=en-GB|issn=0261-3077|last3=Proctor|first3=Kate|last4=Weaver|first4=Matthew|last5=Carrell|first5=Severin}}</ref>

===Benn Bill (4 September 2019)===
{{main|European Union (Withdrawal) (No. 2) Act 2019}}

Once Parliament reconvened from summer recess, Labour MP [[Hilary Benn]] presented a [[European Union (Withdrawal) (No. 6) Bill 2019|bill]] that would rule out a unilateral no-deal Brexit by forcing the Government to reach an Agreement, get parliamentary approval for no-deal Brexit, or, if neither condition is fulfilled by 19 October, then extend the deadline until 31 January 2020.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.nytimes.com/aponline/2019/09/02/world/europe/ap-eu-brexit-parliament.html|title=No-Deal? Intense Brexit Debate Expected in UK Parliament|last=Press|first=The Associated|date=2019-09-02|work=The New York Times|access-date=2019-09-04|language=en-US|issn=0362-4331}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.itv.com/news/2019-09-02/opposition-mps-to-demand-three-month-brexit-extension-in-emergency-debate/|title=Details of 'last chance' Bill to block no-deal Brexit revealed|website=ITV News|language=en|access-date=2019-09-04}}</ref>

On 3 September, [[Oliver Letwin]] submitted a motion for an emergency debate on this bill, in accordance with Standing Order No. 24. This motion, to allow the debate for the following day, passed, 328 to 301.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.itv.com/news/2019-09-02/what-is-the-so24-process-standing-order-24-emergency-debates/|title=Explained: The SO24 process which MPs could use to try to thwart Boris Johnson|website=ITV News|language=en|access-date=2019-09-04}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.euronews.com/2019/09/02/what-is-standing-order-24-euronews-answers|title=What is Standing Order 24?|last=Crowcroft|first=Orlando|date=2019-09-02|website=euronews|language=en|access-date=2019-09-04}}</ref> 21 Conservative MPs voted for the motion and were then removed from the Conservative whip and deselected for future elections, as Johnson had threatened to do in advance. The 21 MPs were [[Guto Bebb]], [[Richard Benyon]], [[Steve Brine]], [[Alistair Burt|Alastair Burt]], [[Greg Clark]], [[Kenneth Clarke|Ken Clarke]], [[David Gauke]], [[Justine Greening]], [[Dominic Grieve]], [[Sam Gyimah]], [[Philip Hammond|Phillip Hammond]], [[Stephen Hammond]], [[Richard Harrington (politician)|Richard Harrington]], [[Margot James]], Letwin, [[Anne Milton]], [[Caroline Nokes]], [[Antoinette Sandbach]], [[Nicholas Soames]], [[Rory Stewart]] and [[Ed Vaizey]]. This, combined with [[Phillip Lee (politician)|Phillip Lee]]’s defection to the Liberal Democrats earlier that day, gave the Opposition a 43-seat majority over the Government.

Johnson further announced his intention to seek an early general election. Under the [[Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011|Fixed-Term Parliaments Act]], a two-thirds majority of the House of Commons is necessary for this motion to pass, but opposition leaders have indicated that they will refuse support until after Benn’s bill is passed.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/sep/03/boris-johnson-suffers-commons-defeat-as-tories-turn-against-him|title=Boris Johnson to seek election after rebel Tories deliver Commons defeat|last1=Stewart|first1=Heather|date=2019-09-03|work=The Guardian|access-date=2019-09-04|last2=Walker|first2=Peter|language=en-GB|issn=0261-3077}}</ref>

On 4 September, the Benn Bill passed second reading by 329 to 300; a 22nd Conservative, [[Caroline Spelman]], voted against the Government position.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/2019/09/04/no-deal-brexit-vote-tory-rebels-voted-against-boris-johnson/|title=No-deal Brexit vote: See how your MP voted as list of Tory rebels grows|last1=Kirk|first1=Ashley|date=2019-09-04|work=The Telegraph|access-date=2019-09-04|last2=Scott|first2=Patrick|language=en-GB|issn=0307-1235}}</ref> Later the same day MPs subsequently rejected Johnson's motion to call an October general election, failing to achieve the two-thirds Commons majority needed under the [[Fixed-term Parliaments Act]], in a vote of 298 to 56. [[Labour Party (UK)|Labour]] MPs abstained from the vote.<ref>{{cite news |title=Boris Johnson's call for general election rejected by MPs |date=4 September 2019 |work=BBC News |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49584907 |access-date=4 September 2019}}</ref>

On 9 September, the Benn Bill was granted [[royal assent]].<ref>{{cite news|url=https://metro.co.uk/2019/09/09/no-deal-brexit-officially-blocked-law-10711993/ |title=No-deal Brexit officially blocked in law |work=Metro |date=9 September 2019 |access-date=9 September 2019}}</ref> On the same day, MPs backed a motion calling for the publication of all government communications relating to no-deal Brexit planning and the suspension of Parliament, voting 311 to 302.<ref>[https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-boris-johnson-parliament-no-deal-vote-operation-yellowhammer-dominic-cummings-a9098311.html Brexit: MPs order Boris Johnson to hand over government communications about parliament suspension and no-deal planning], by Ashley Cowburn, Sept 9, 2019, Independent.co.uk website.</ref> A second government motion calling for an early general election failed to achieve the required super-majority, with 293 MPs voting in favour of it.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49630094 |title=Brexit: Boris Johnson's second attempt to trigger election fails |work=BBC News|date=10 September 2019 |access-date=10 September 2019}}</ref> Parliament was then prorogued until 14 October.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49645338 |title=Brexit: Protests as five-week Parliament suspension begins |work=BBC News |publisher=BBC |date=10 September 2019 |access-date=10 September 2019}}</ref> The prorogation was subsequently overturned on 24 September following [[R (Miller) v The Prime Minister and Cherry v Advocate General for Scotland|legal challenges against the government]],<ref>{{cite news |last= Bowcott |first=Owen |date=24 September 2019 |title=Boris Johnson's suspension of parliament unlawful, supreme court rules |url=https://www.theguardian.com/law/2019/sep/24/boris-johnsons-suspension-of-parliament-unlawful-supreme-court-rules-prorogue |work=The Guardian |access-date=24 September 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |title=Supreme Court: Suspending Parliament was unlawful, judges rule |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49810261 |work=BBC News |date=24 September 2019 |access-date=24 September 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite court |litigants=R (Miller) (Appellant) v The Prime Minister (Respondent) and Cherry & Ors (Respondents) v Advocate General for Scotland (Appellant) (Scotland) |reporter=&#91;2019&#93; UKSC 41 |date=24 September 2019 |url=https://www.supremecourt.uk/cases/docs/uksc-2019-0192-judgment.pdf |access-date=24 September 2018}}</ref> and parliament reconvened the following day,<ref>{{cite news |title=Johnson defends language after criticism from MPs |url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-49843363 |work=BBC News |date=26 September 2019 |access-date=26 September 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news |title=Boris Johnson refuses to apologise for language about Jo Cox |url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/sep/26/boris-johnson-refuses-to-apologise-for-language-about-jo-cox |work=The Guardian |date=26 September 2019 |access-date=26 September 2019}}</ref> with a shorter prorogation then taking place for six days, from 8 to 14 October.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.standard.co.uk/news/politics/parliament-shutdown-begins-after-calm-ceremony-as-threat-of-nodeal-brexit-looms-a4257186.html |title=UK Parliament prorogation: Shutdown begins after calm ceremony as threat of no-deal Brexit looms |newspaper=London Evening Standard |date=8 October 2019 |access-date=9 October 2019}}</ref>

=== Second Letwin amendment (19 October 2019) ===
Following further negotiations between the UK and EU, a [[Brexit withdrawal agreement#Revisions in 2019|revised withdrawal agreement]] was reached on 17 October.<ref>{{cite news|first=Holly |last=Ellyatt |url=https://www.cnbc.com/2019/10/17/uk-and-eu-agree-on-new-brexit-deal-boris-johnson-says.html |title=UK and EU agree on new Brexit deal, Boris Johnson says |publisher=NBC |work=CNBC |date=17 October 2019 |access-date=19 October 2019}}</ref> A special Saturday sitting of Parliament (dubbed "Super Saturday" by the media) was held two days later to debate the new agreement.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-49984367|title=Brexit: Special sitting for MPs to decide UK's future|date=9 October 2019|work=BBC News|access-date=9 October 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/oct/09/parliament-set-for-brexit-showdown-on-19-october|title=Parliament set for Brexit showdown on 19 October|last=Murphy|first=Simon|date=9 October 2019|work=The Guardian|access-date=9 October 2019}}</ref><ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/oct/18/brexit-super-saturday-will-the-deal-pass |title=Brexit 'super Saturday': your guide to the big day |publisher=Guardian Media Group |newspaper=The Guardian |date=19 October 2019 |access-date=19 October 2019}}</ref> MPs passed the second Letwin amendment 322 to 306, which withheld Parliament's approval until legislation implementing the deal has been passed, and forced the Government to request the EU for a delay to Brexit until 31 January 2020.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/oct/19/mps-put-brakes-on-boris-johnsons-brexit-deal-with-rebel-letwin-amendment|title=MPs put brakes on Boris Johnson's Brexit deal with rebel amendment|last1=Stewart|first1=Heather|date=2019-10-19|work=The Guardian|access-date=2019-10-19|last2=Proctor|first2=Kate|language=en-GB|issn=0261-3077}}</ref> The amended motion was then passed by MPs without a vote as the Government effectively accepted defeat.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-50095368 |title=Saturday's Brexit vote in Parliament: What happens now? |work=BBC News |date=22 October 2019 |access-date=22 October 2019}}</ref> On 21 October the [[Speaker of the House of Commons (United Kingdom)|Speaker of the House of Commons]] [[John Bercow]] refused a government request to hold a vote on the Brexit deal, citing their previous decision to withdraw it.<ref>{{Cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-50128740|title=MPs' vote on Brexit deal ruled out by Speaker|date=21 October 2019|work=BBC News|access-date=21 October 2019|language=en-GB}}</ref>

On the evening of 19 October, 10 Downing Street confirmed that Boris Johnson would send a letter to the EU requesting an extension, but would not sign it.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-50112924 |title=Brexit: PM sends letter to Brussels seeking further delay |work=BBC News |date=19 October 2019 |access-date=19 October 2019}}</ref> EU Council President [[Donald Tusk]] subsequently confirmed receipt of the letter, which Johnson had described as "Parliament's letter, not my letter". In addition, Johnson sent a second letter expressing the view that any further delay to Brexit would be a mistake.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-50112924 |title=Brexit: PM sends letter to Brussels seeking further delay |work=BBC News |publisher=BBC |date=20 October 2019 |access-date=20 October 2019}}</ref>
[[File:Second Letwin Amendment Result.jpg|thumb|Results of the division announced at 14:50. Tellers from left to right: [[Matt Western]], [[Thangam Debbonaire]], [[Stuart Andrew]] and [[Iain Stewart (politician)|Iain Stewart]].]]

=== Withdrawal Agreement Debate (22 October 2019) ===
On 21 October, the government published the Withdrawal Agreement Bill and proposed three days of debate for opposition MPs to scrutinise it.<ref>{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-50132790|title=Government publishes Brexit bill|website=[[Bbc.co.uk/news|BBC.co.uk/News]]|publisher=[[BBC News]]|date=21 October 2019|access-date=22 October 2019|language=en-GB|df=dmy-all}}</ref> The government brought the recently revised EU Withdrawal Bill to the House of Commons for debate on the evening of 22 October 2019.<ref name="BBC22Oct">{{cite news|url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-50146182|title=MPs reject Brexit bill timetable|website=[[Bbc.co.uk/news|BBC.co.uk/News]]|publisher=[[BBC News]]|date=22 October 2019|access-date=22 October 2019|language=en-GB|df=dmy-all}}</ref> MPs voted to give the Bill a second reading which was passed by 329 votes to 299, and the timetable for debating the Bill, which was defeated by 322 votes to 308. Prior to the votes, Johnson had stated that if his timetable failed to generate the support needed to pass in parliament he would abandon attempts to get the deal approved and would seek a general election. Following the vote Johnson announced that the legislation would be paused while he consulted with other EU leaders.<ref name="BBC22Oct"/><ref name="Reuters22Oct"/>

=== Technical Withdrawal Amendments (23 October 2019) ===
On 23 October, the House of Commons debated three technical pieces of legislation relating to the UK's withdrawal from the EU. The legislation debate addressed the repeal of certain technical provisions enshrined in UK law regarding the EU. Were the vote to pass for these three acts they would only come into effect if the UK were to ultimately leave the EU. The three items debated dealt with amendments to existing UK law in order to repeal 1) the freedom of movement provisions of the EU<ref name="ReutersFreeMov">{{cite news|url=https://uk.reuters.com/article/britain-eu-immigration/uk-plans-to-end-eu-freedom-of-movement-immediately-in-no-deal-brexit-idUKL5N25F45D|title=UK plans to end EU freedom of movement immediately in no-deal Brexit|website=[[Reuters.com]]|publisher=[[Reuters]]|date=19 August 2019|access-date=24 October 2019|language=en-GB|df=dmy-all}}</ref><ref name="LegislationGov1">{{cite web|url=http://www.legislation.gov.uk/ukdsi/2019/9780111188545/note|title=The Freedom of Establishment and Free Movement of Services (EU Exit) Regulations 2019|website=[[Legislation.gov.uk]]|publisher=[[UK Government]]|access-date=24 October 2019|language=en-GB|df=dmy-all}}</ref> 2) regulatory oversight of the UK by third party (EU) countries<ref name="LegislationGov2">{{cite web|url=http://www.legislation.gov.uk/uksi/2019/177/note|title=The Statutory Auditors and Third Country Auditors (Amendment) (EU Exit) Regulations 2019|website=[[Legislation.gov.uk]]|publisher=[[UK Government]]|date=1 February 2019|access-date=24 October 2019|language=en-GB|df=dmy-all}}</ref> 3) regulations on financial services codified by the EU.<ref name="LegislationGov3">{{cite web|url=http://www.legislation.gov.uk/uksi/2019/1390/pdfs/uksi_20191390_en.pdf|title=The Financial Services (Miscellaneous) (Amendment) (EU Exit) (No. 3) Regulations 2019|website=[[Legislation.gov.uk]]|publisher=[[UK Government]]|date=24 October 2019|access-date=24 October 2019|language=en-GB|df=dmy-all}}</ref> All three amendments went to a division vote, and all three passed the House of Commons vote.<ref name="ComVotes5"/><ref name="ComVotes6"/><ref name="ComVotes7"/>

==Votes during the 58th Parliament of the United Kingdom (2019–24)==
{{See also|2019 UK General Election}}

=== Withdrawal Agreement Debate (20 December 2019) ===
On 20 December 2019, immediately after the state opening of Parliament following the [[2019 United Kingdom general election]] (in which the Conservative Party secured a large majority of 80 seats), the Government presented another Bill to ratify its draft withdrawal agreement. It also proposed another [[Cloture#United Kingdom|guillotine motion]] to curtail debate on the Bill.
